     <p>
      <b>
       David
      </b>
      (
      <span class="nowrap">
       <span class="IPA nopopups">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">
         /
         <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
          <span title="// primary stress follows">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
           d
          </span>
          <span title="/e/ long 'a' in 'base'">
           e
          </span>
          <span title="'v' in 'vie'">
           v
          </span>
          <span title="// short 'i' in 'bid'">
           
          </span>
          <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
           d
          </span>
         </span>
         /
        </a>
       </span>
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">
       Hebrew
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="he" style="white-space:nowrap;font-size:125%" xml:lang="he">
       
      </span>
      ,
      <small>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Israeli_Hebrew" title="Modern Israeli Hebrew">
        Modern
       </a>
      </small>
      <i>
       <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="he transliteration">
        David
       </span>
      </i>
      ,
      <small>
       <a href="/wiki/Tiberian_vocalization" title="Tiberian vocalization">
        Tiberian
       </a>
      </small>
      <i>
       <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Hebrew transliteration">
        Dw
       </span>
      </i>
      ;
      <small>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_259-3" title="ISO 259-3">
        ISO 259-3
       </a>
      </small>
      <i>
       Dawid
      </i>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
       Arabic
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="ar" xml:lang="ar">
       
      </span>
      
      <i>
       <span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="DIN 31635 Arabic">
        Dwd
       </span>
      </i>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">
       Syriac
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="syr" xml:lang="syr">
       
      </span>
      <i>
       Dawid
      </i>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">
       AncientGreek
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
       Greek
      </a>
      :
      <span lang="el" xml:lang="el">
       
      </span>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Strong%27s_Concordance" title="Strong's Concordance">
       Strong's
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       Daveed
      </i>
      ) was, according to
      <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Samuel" title="Books of Samuel">
       the books of Samuel
      </a>
      , the second king of the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">
       United Kingdom of Israel
      </a>
      , and according to the
      <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">
       Gospels of Matthew
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Luke" title="Gospel of Luke">
       Luke
      </a>
      , an ancestor of
      <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      . His life is conventionally dated to c. 1040970 BCE, his reign over
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Judah" title="Kingdom of Judah">
       Judah
      </a>
      c. 1010970 BCE.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STIC_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-STIC-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Israel_Finkelstein" title="Israel Finkelstein">
       Israel Finkelstein
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Neil_Asher_Silberman" title="Neil Asher Silberman">
       Neil Asher Silberman
      </a>
      reject the idea that David ruled over a united monarchy, suggesting instead that he ruled only as a chieftain over the southern kingdom of Judah, much smaller than the northern kingdom of Israel at that time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They note that Israel and Judah were still polytheistic in the time of David and Solomon, and posit that much later seventh century redactors sought to portray a past golden age of a united, monotheistic monarchy in order to serve contemporary needs. The lack of evidence for David's military campaigns and the relative underdevelopment of Jerusalem, the capital of Judah, compared to the highly developed and urbanized Samaria, capital of Israel, further reinforce this view.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Samuel" title="Books of Samuel">
       Books of Samuel
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">
       1 Kings
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Chronicles" title="Books of Chronicles">
       1 Chronicles
      </a>
      are the only
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
       Old Testament
      </a>
      sources of information on David, although the
      <a href="/wiki/Tel_Dan_Stele" title="Tel Dan Stele">
       Tel Dan Stele
      </a>
      (dated c. 850835 BCE) contains the phrase
      <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL Hebrew', David">
        
      </span>
      (bytdwd), read as "House of David", which many scholars confirm to be a likely
      <a href="/wiki/Plausible_reasoning" title="Plausible reasoning">
       plausible
      </a>
      match to the existence in the mid-9th century BCE of a
      <a href="/wiki/Judea_and_Samaria_Area" title="Judea and Samaria Area">
       Judean
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">
       royal dynasty
      </a>
      called the
      <a href="/wiki/Davidic_line" title="Davidic line">
       House of David
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlter2004xii_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlter2004xii-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Depicted as an acclaimed courageous warrior, and a
      <a href="/wiki/Poet" title="Poet">
       poet
      </a>
      and musician credited for composing much of the psalms contained in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Psalms" title="Book of Psalms">
       Book of Psalms
      </a>
      , King David is widely viewed as a
      <a href="/wiki/Righteous_indignation" title="Righteous indignation">
       righteous
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Effectiveness" title="Effectiveness">
       effective
      </a>
      king in battle and
      <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">
       civil justice
      </a>
      . He is described as a man after God's own heart in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1+Samuel+13%3A14&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        1 Samuel 13:14
       </a>
      </span>
      and
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+13%3A22&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        Acts 13:22
       </a>
      </span>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      David is an important figure to members of the Jewish, Christian, and Islamic faiths. Biblical tradition maintains the
      <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">
       Messiah
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Davidic_line" title="Davidic line">
       direct descent
      </a>
      from the line of David. In Islam, David is considered a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" title="Prophets of Islam">
       prophet
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_narrative">
       Biblical narrative
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biblical narrative">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Saul_rejected">
       Saul rejected
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Saul rejected">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Saul" title="Saul">
       Saul
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Samuel_e_david.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="672" data-file-width="584" height="253" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Samuel_e_david.jpg/220px-Samuel_e_david.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Samuel_e_david.jpg/330px-Samuel_e_david.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Samuel_e_david.jpg/440px-Samuel_e_david.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Samuel_e_david.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samuel_(Bible)" title="Samuel (Bible)">
         Samuel
        </a>
        anoints David,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dura_Europos" title="Dura Europos">
         Dura Europos
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">
         Syria
        </a>
        , 3rd century CE
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <span id="childhood">
      </span>
      <span id="Jesse">
      </span>
      <span id="Bethlehem">
      </span>
      According to the Biblical narrative, God appointed
      <a href="/wiki/Saul" title="Saul">
       Saul
      </a>
      to be the first king of Israel, after the leading elders of the land demanded a king to replace the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_Judges" title="Biblical Judges">
       Judges
      </a>
      who had previously ruled the country. Although successful at first, Saul eventually lost favor with God by disobeying directives conveyed through the prophet Samuel. Samuel told Saul that God had rejected him as king, and would give the kingdom instead to "a man after [my] own heart"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who was "better than you."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Samuel" title="Samuel">
       Samuel
      </a>
      went to Bethlehem on the pretext of performing a sacrifice lest Saul discover his real purpose. He sought a new king from among the sons of
      <a href="/wiki/Jesse" title="Jesse">
       Jesse
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">
       Bethlehem
      </a>
      . Samuel examined seven of Jesse's sons, but said to him, "The Lord has not chosen these. Samuel inquired if Jesse had any other sons. Jesse indicated that David, the youngest, was tending the flocks. Samuel said, "Send for him," and had him brought in. Then the Lord said, "Rise and anoint him; this is the one." So Samuel took the horn of oil and anointed him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chabad_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-chabad-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="At_Saul.27s_court">
       At Saul's court
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: At Saul's court">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Bible presents two accounts of how David came to become part of Sauls household. In 1 Samuel 16:14-23, Saul takes David into his service as his armor-bearer. In 1 Samuel 17, David is officially presented to Saul as the hero who killed Goliath. According to French scholar, Andre Lemaire, this may represent an amalgamation of different traditions concerning the early relationship between David and Saul.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemaire_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lemaire-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Saul was tormented by an "evil spirit from the Lord" which plunged him into deep melancholy. It was suggested that music might ease his mind, so he sent for David, who was known for playing the lyre. Saul made David one of his armor-bearers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corbett_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-corbett-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From then on, whenever the spirit from God came on Saul, David would take up his lyre and play. Then relief would come to Saul; he would feel better, and the evil spirit would leave him.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="David_and_Goliath">
       David and Goliath
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: David and Goliath">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="1280" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a7/David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg/240px-David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a7/David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg/360px-David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a7/David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg/480px-David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_and_Goliath_-1700s.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        "David Giving Thanks to God After the Death of Goliath", 18th century painting attributed to
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Errard_the_Younger" title="Charles Errard the Younger">
         Charles Errard the Younger
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Goliath" title="Goliath">
       Goliath
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      According to 1 Samuel 17, the 'men of Israel' under King
      <a href="/wiki/Saul" title="Saul">
       Saul
      </a>
      faced the
      <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">
       Philistines
      </a>
      near the
      <a href="/wiki/Valley_of_Elah" title="Valley of Elah">
       Valley of Elah
      </a>
      . David had been sent by his father to bring provisions to his brothers with the army. He heard the Philistine giant
      <a href="/wiki/Goliath" title="Goliath">
       Goliath
      </a>
      challenge the
      <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
       Israelites
      </a>
      to send their own champion to decide the outcome in single combat, and was overheard commenting that the uncircumcised Philistine should not insult the army of the living God. Brought to the king, he expressed confidence that he could defeat Goliath just as he had killed a lion and a bear threatening the flock. Saul reluctantly let David face Goliath.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="500" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg/220px-The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg/330px-The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg/440px-The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Triumph_of_David.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        David holds the impaled head of Goliath and marches before a general on a white horse, as envisioned by Poussin, ca. 1632
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      David picked five smooth stones from a nearby brook, and struck Goliath in the forehead with a stone from his
      <a href="/wiki/Sling_(weapon)" title="Sling (weapon)">
       sling
      </a>
      . Goliath fell dead, and David took Goliath's sword and beheaded him. The
      <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">
       Philistines
      </a>
      fled in terror. Saul inquired about the name of the young champion and David told him that he was the son of
      <a href="/wiki/Jesse" title="Jesse">
       Jesse
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corbett_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-corbett-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Samuel" title="Books of Samuel">
       2 Samuel
      </a>
      22, David credited God for delivering him from the hand of the Philistines and saving him from "the snares of death," in his psalm, "Davids Song of Praise."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Hebrew texts of
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2+Samuel+21%3A19&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        2 Samuel 21:19
       </a>
      </span>
      mention a man named Goliath being killed by
      <a href="/wiki/Elhanan_son_of_Jair" title="Elhanan son of Jair">
       Elhanan
      </a>
      son of
      <a href="/wiki/Jair" title="Jair">
       Jair
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">
       Bethlehem
      </a>
      (1 Chronicles 20:5 retells this story using Goliath's
      <i>
       brother
      </i>
      ).
      <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Halpern" title="Baruch Halpern">
       Baruch Halpern
      </a>
      believes that, "most likely, storytellers displaced the deed from the otherwise obscure Elhanan onto the more famous character, David."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="David_and_Jonathan">
       David and Jonathan
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: David and Jonathan">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jusepe_Leonardo_001.jpeg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="360" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jusepe_Leonardo_001.jpeg/220px-Jusepe_Leonardo_001.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Jusepe_Leonardo_001.jpeg/330px-Jusepe_Leonardo_001.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Jusepe_Leonardo_001.jpeg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jusepe_Leonardo_001.jpeg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Saul threatening David, by
        <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Leonardo" title="Jos Leonardo">
         Jos Leonardo
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/David_and_Jonathan" title="David and Jonathan">
       David and Jonathan
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Saul made David a commander over his armies and offered him his daughter
      <a href="/wiki/Michal" title="Michal">
       Michal
      </a>
      in marriage for bringing 100
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreskins" title="Foreskins">
       foreskins
      </a>
      of the Philistines but David brought back 200, saying "God was with me". David was successful in many battles, and his popularity awakened Saul's fears. Saul tried to arrange for David's death, but the plots only endeared David further to the people, and especially to his friend, Saul's son
      <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_(1_Samuel)" title="Jonathan (1 Samuel)">
       Jonathan
      </a>
      . (1 Samuel 18:1, 2 Samuel 1:2526).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jonathan warned David, who fled into the wilderness. As one Saul's commanders, he had already developed a cadre of well-trained, loyal soldiers who followed him into the wilderness.
     </p>
     <p>
      He accepted the bordertown of
      <a href="/wiki/Ziklag" title="Ziklag">
       Ziklag
      </a>
      from the Philistine king
      <a href="/wiki/Achish" title="Achish">
       Achish
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Gath_(city)" title="Gath (city)">
       Gath
      </a>
      , but maintained good relations with the leaders of the territory of Judah by fighting the Amalekites.
      <a href="/wiki/Achish" title="Achish">
       Achish
      </a>
      marched against Saul, but David was excused from the war because the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philistine" title="Philistine">
       Philistine
      </a>
      nobles did not trust him.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Proclaimed_king">
       Proclaimed king
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Proclaimed king">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Jonathan and Saul were killed in battle with the Philistines at
      <a href="/wiki/Mount_Gilboa" title="Mount Gilboa">
       Mount Gilboa
      </a>
      . David mourned their deaths, especially that of Jonathan. He travelled to
      <a href="/wiki/Hebron" title="Hebron">
       Hebron
      </a>
      , where he was
      <a href="/wiki/Anointing" title="Anointing">
       anointed
      </a>
      king. In the north, Saul's son
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ish-Bosheth" title="Ish-Bosheth">
       Ish-Bosheth
      </a>
      was anointed by
      <a href="/wiki/Abner" title="Abner">
       Abner
      </a>
      as king. War ensued between Ish-Bosheth and David, until Ish-Bosheth was murdered. The assassins brought the head of Ish-Bosheth to David hoping for a reward, but David executed them for their crime. With the death of Saul's son, the elders of Israel came to
      <a href="/wiki/Hebron" title="Hebron">
       Hebron
      </a>
      and David was anointed King over
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">
       a united Israel
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Jerusalem_and_the_Davidic_covenant">
       Jerusalem and the Davidic covenant
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Jerusalem and the Davidic covenant">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Covenant_(biblical)#Davidic_covenant" title="Covenant (biblical)">
       Covenant (biblical)  Davidic covenant
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      David conquered the
      <a href="/wiki/Jebusite" title="Jebusite">
       Jebusite
      </a>
      fortress of
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      , and made it his capital. David brought the
      <a href="/wiki/Ark_of_the_Covenant" title="Ark of the Covenant">
       Ark of the Covenant
      </a>
      to Jerusalem, intending to build a temple. The prophet
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nathan_(Prophet)" title="Nathan (Prophet)">
       Nathan
      </a>
      , announced that the temple would be built at a future date by one of David's sons (Solomon). Nathan told David that God had made a covenant with David, promising to establish the house of David: "Your throne shall be established forever." David won victories over the Philistines, and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moabites" title="Moabites">
       Moabites
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemaire_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lemaire-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hadadezer" title="Hadadezer">
       Hadadezer
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Zobah" title="Zobah">
       Zobah
      </a>
      paid tribute.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bathsheba_and_Uriah_the_Hittite">
       Bathsheba and Uriah the Hittite
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bathsheba and Uriah the Hittite">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="455" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg/220px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg/330px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg/440px-Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paris_psaulter_gr139_fol7v.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        King David in robes of a Byzantine emperor, miniature from the
        <a href="/wiki/Paris_Psalter" title="Paris Psalter">
         Paris Psalter
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      David committed
      <a href="/wiki/Adultery#Biblical_sources" title="Adultery">
       adultery
      </a>
      with
      <a href="/wiki/Bathsheba" title="Bathsheba">
       Bathsheba
      </a>
      , the wife of
      <a href="/wiki/Uriah_the_Hittite" title="Uriah the Hittite">
       Uriah the Hittite
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bathsheba became pregnant. David sent for Uriah, who was with the Israelite army at the siege of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rabbah" title="Rabbah">
       Rabbah
      </a>
      , hoping Uriah would sleep with his wife and, thus, be believed to be the child's father. Uriah refused to stay at his home while his companions were in the field of battle, so David sent him back to
      <a href="/wiki/Joab" title="Joab">
       Joab
      </a>
      , the commander, with a message instructing Joab to ensure that Uriah died in battle. Joab placed Uriah on the front lines and had the other soldiers retreat from the area. Uriah was killed which allowed David to marry Bathsheba. She then bore his child, "but the thing that David had done displeased the Lord."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The prophet
      <a href="/wiki/Nathan_(prophet)" title="Nathan (prophet)">
       Nathan
      </a>
      confronted David, saying: "Why have you despised the word of God, to do what is evil in his sight? You have smitten Uriah the Hittite with the sword, and have taken his wife to be your wife." Nathan conveys three consequences of David's actions from God: First, that the "sword shall never depart from your house" (2 Samuel 12:10); second, that "Before your very eyes I will take your wives and give them to one who is close to you, and he will sleep with your wives in broad daylight", and finally, that "the son born to you will die" (
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=2%20Samuel&amp;verse=12:14&amp;src=KJV">
       2Samuel 12:14
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      David humbles himself, but the child dies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      David then leaves his lamentations, dresses himself, goes to the House of the Lord and worships, and finally returns home to eat. His servants ask why he wept when the baby was alive, but ended his mourning when the child died. David replies: "While the child was still alive, I fasted and wept. I thought, 'Who knows? The L
      <span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">
       ORD
      </span>
      may be gracious to me and let the child live.' But now that he is dead, why should I fast? Can I bring him back again? I will go to him, but he will not return to me." (
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=2%20Samuel&amp;verse=12:2223&amp;src=KJV">
       2Samuel 12:2223
      </a>
      )
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="David.27s_son_Absalom_rebels">
       David's son Absalom rebels
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: David's son Absalom rebels">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="1050" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg/220px-David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg/330px-David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg/440px-David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_Playing_the_Harp_1670_Jan_de_Bray.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         David Playing the Harp
        </i>
        , by
        <a href="/wiki/Jan_de_Bray" title="Jan de Bray">
         Jan de Bray
        </a>
        , 1670
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      David's son
      <a href="/wiki/Absalom" title="Absalom">
       Absalom
      </a>
      rebelled, forcing David to flee Jerusalem as the kingdom plunged into civil war. However, David sent his servant
      <a href="/wiki/Hushai" title="Hushai">
       Hushai
      </a>
      to Absalom's court as a
      <a href="/wiki/Double_agent" title="Double agent">
       double agent
      </a>
      both to thwart the counsel of Absalom's chief adviser, fellow traitor
      <a href="/wiki/Ahitophel" title="Ahitophel">
       Ahitophel
      </a>
      and relay intelligence to David's forces. Hushai is successful in persuading Absalom from immediately pursuing his father in favor of better preparing Absalom's own forces for a major battle, thus allowing David to regroup for it. In the battle of the
      <a href="/wiki/Wood_of_Ephraim" title="Wood of Ephraim">
       Wood of Ephraim
      </a>
      , Absalom's forces were defeated, and his head was caught in the branches of a
      <a href="/wiki/Terebinth" title="Terebinth">
       terebinth
      </a>
      , and Davids general Joab killed Absalom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When the news of the victory was brought to David, he was grief-stricken, and he cried out "O my son Absalom, my son, my son Absalom! Would I had died instead of you, O Absalom, my son, my son!"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Death">
       Death
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Death">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="956" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg/290px-Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg/435px-Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg/580px-Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg 2x" width="290"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Royal_Funeral007.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The funeral of King David, while his son
        <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">
         Solomon
        </a>
        watches (from a medieval manuscript)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      When David had become old and bedridden,
      <a href="/wiki/Adonijah" title="Adonijah">
       Adonijah
      </a>
      , his eldest surviving son and natural heir, declared himself king. Bathsheba, David's favorite wife, and
      <a href="/wiki/Nathan_(prophet)" title="Nathan (prophet)">
       Nathan the prophet
      </a>
      went to David and obtained his agreement that Bathsheba's son
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">
       Solomon
      </a>
      should become king. David counseled his son to kill all those who had been his enemies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Family">
       Family
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Family">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      According to tradition, David was born in
      <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">
       Bethlehem
      </a>
      , in the territory of the
      <a href="/wiki/Tribe_of_Judah" title="Tribe of Judah">
       Tribe of Judah
      </a>
      . His grandfather was
      <a href="/wiki/Obed_(biblical_figure)" title="Obed (biblical figure)">
       Obed
      </a>
      , whose mother was the Moabite
      <a href="/wiki/Ruth_(biblical_figure)" title="Ruth (biblical figure)">
       Ruth
      </a>
      and whose grandmother was the former prostitute
      <a href="/wiki/Rahab" title="Rahab">
       Rahab
      </a>
      . David's father was
      <a href="/wiki/Jesse" title="Jesse">
       Jesse
      </a>
      . His mother is not named in the Bible, but the
      <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">
       Talmud
      </a>
      identifies her as
      <a href="/wiki/Nitzevet" title="Nitzevet">
       Nitzevet
      </a>
      daughter of Adael.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      David had six older brothers and two sisters,
      <a href="/wiki/Zeruiah" title="Zeruiah">
       Zeruiah
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Abigail_(mother_of_Amasa)" title="Abigail (mother of Amasa)">
       Abigail
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      David cemented his relations with various political and national groups through marriage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lemaire_7-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lemaire-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He had eight wives:
      <a href="/wiki/Michal" title="Michal">
       Michal
      </a>
      , the second daughter of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Saul" title="King Saul">
       King Saul
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Ahinoam" title="Ahinoam">
       Ahinoam
      </a>
      the Jezreelite;
      <a href="/wiki/Abigail" title="Abigail">
       Abigail
      </a>
      the Carmelite, previously wife of
      <a href="/wiki/Nabal" title="Nabal">
       Nabal
      </a>
      ;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maachah" title="Maachah">
       Maachah
      </a>
      , daughter of
      <a href="/wiki/Talmai" title="Talmai">
       Talmai
      </a>
      , king of
      <a href="/wiki/Geshur" title="Geshur">
       Geshur
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Haggith" title="Haggith">
       Haggith
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Abital" title="Abital">
       Abital
      </a>
      ;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eglah" title="Eglah">
       Eglah
      </a>
      ; and
      <a href="/wiki/Bathsheba" title="Bathsheba">
       Bathsheba
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Chronicles" title="Book of Chronicles">
       Book of Chronicles
      </a>
      lists his sons by various
      <a href="/wiki/King_David%27s_wives" title="King David's wives">
       wives
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubine" title="Concubine">
       concubines
      </a>
      . In
      <a href="/wiki/Hebron" title="Hebron">
       Hebron
      </a>
      , David had six sons:
      <a href="/wiki/Amnon" title="Amnon">
       Amnon
      </a>
      , by
      <a href="/wiki/Ahinoam" title="Ahinoam">
       Ahinoam
      </a>
      ;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daniel_(son_of_David)" title="Daniel (son of David)">
       Daniel
      </a>
      , by
      <a href="/wiki/Abigail" title="Abigail">
       Abigail
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Absalom" title="Absalom">
       Absalom
      </a>
      , by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maachah" title="Maachah">
       Maachah
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Adonijah" title="Adonijah">
       Adonijah
      </a>
      , by
      <a href="/wiki/Haggith" title="Haggith">
       Haggith
      </a>
      ;
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shephatiah" title="Shephatiah">
       Shephatiah
      </a>
      , by
      <a href="/wiki/Abital" title="Abital">
       Abital
      </a>
      ; and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ithream" title="Ithream">
       Ithream
      </a>
      , by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eglah" title="Eglah">
       Eglah
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By Bathsheba, his sons were
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shammua" title="Shammua">
       Shammua
      </a>
      , Shobab,
      <a href="/wiki/Nathan_(son_of_David)" title="Nathan (son of David)">
       Nathan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">
       Solomon
      </a>
      . David's sons born in Jerusalem by other wives included
      <a href="/wiki/Ibhar" title="Ibhar">
       Ibhar
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elishua" title="Elishua">
       Elishua
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eliphelet" title="Eliphelet">
       Eliphelet
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nogah" title="Nogah">
       Nogah
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nepheg" title="Nepheg">
       Nepheg
      </a>
      , Japhia,
      <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Elishama" title="Elishama">
       Elishama
      </a>
      and Eliada.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Jerimoth" title="Jerimoth">
       Jerimoth
      </a>
      , who is not mentioned in any of the genealogies, is mentioned as another of his sons in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2+Chronicles+11%3A18&amp;version=NIV" rel="nofollow">
        2 Chronicles 11:18
       </a>
      </span>
      . David also had at least one daughter,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamar_(2_Samuel)" title="Tamar (2 Samuel)">
       Tamar
      </a>
      , by Maachah.
     </p>
     <p>
      Given that Nathan prophecies, in punishment for his sin with Bathsheba, that the Lord will take his wives and give them to his "neighbor", Solomon may not have been David's son at all, but later editors amended the story to emphasize Nathan's other statement that "the sword shall never depart from your house".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_17-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Historicity">
       Historicity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Historicity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeology">
       Archaeology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Archaeology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tel_dan_inscription.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="968" data-file-width="1237" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Tel_dan_inscription.png/220px-Tel_dan_inscription.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Tel_dan_inscription.png/330px-Tel_dan_inscription.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Tel_dan_inscription.png/440px-Tel_dan_inscription.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tel_dan_inscription.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Tel_Dan_Stele" title="Tel Dan Stele">
         Tel Dan Stele
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Tel_Dan_Stele" title="Tel Dan Stele">
       Tel Dan Stele
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mesha_Stele" title="Mesha Stele">
       Mesha Stele
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/City_of_David" title="City of David">
       City of David
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Two archaeological finds, the
      <a href="/wiki/Tel_Dan_Stele" title="Tel Dan Stele">
       Tel Dan Stele
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Mesha_Stele" title="Mesha Stele">
       Mesha Stele
      </a>
      , have direct bearing on the question of the existence of a historical David. The first of these is an
      <a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">
       Aramean
      </a>
      victory
      <a href="/wiki/Stele" title="Stele">
       stele
      </a>
      (inscribed stone) discovered in 1993 at
      <a href="/wiki/Dan_(ancient_city)" title="Dan (ancient city)">
       Tel Dan
      </a>
      and dated c. 850835 BCE: it contains the phrase
      <span class="script-hebrew" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL Hebrew', David">
       
      </span>
      (
      <i>
       bytdwd
      </i>
      ), which has been interpreted as "House of David".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenzie_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-McKenzie-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Mesha_Stele" title="Mesha Stele">
       Mesha Stele
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Moab" title="Moab">
       Moab
      </a>
      , dating from approximately the same period, may also contain the name David in line 12, where the interpretation is uncertain, and in line 31, where one destroyed letter must be supplied, but apparently no other letter produces a word that makes sense in the context.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The evidence from surface surveys indicates that Judah at the time of David was a small tribal kingdom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_age" title="Bronze age">
       Bronze
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">
       Iron Age
      </a>
      remains of the
      <a href="/wiki/City_of_David" title="City of David">
       City of David
      </a>
      , the original urban core of Jerusalem identified with the reigns of David and Solomon, were investigated extensively in the 1970s and 1980s under the direction of Yigal Shiloh of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hebrew_University" title="Hebrew University">
       Hebrew University
      </a>
      , who failed to discover significant evidence of occupation during the 10th century BCE.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2005
      <a href="/wiki/Eilat_Mazar" title="Eilat Mazar">
       Eilat Mazar
      </a>
      reported the discovery of a
      <a href="/wiki/Large_Stone_Structure" title="Large Stone Structure">
       Large Stone Structure
      </a>
      which she claimed was David's palace,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but the site is contaminated and cannot be accurately dated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In December 2014, archaeologists from
      <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_State_University" title="Mississippi State University">
       Mississippi State University
      </a>
      announced the discovery of six
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bulla_(seal)" title="Bulla (seal)">
        bullae
       </a>
      </i>
      which suggests that some type of government activity was being conducted in the 10th century, and thus makes the existence of David possible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Academic_views_on_the_biblical_account">
       Academic views on the biblical account
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Academic views on the biblical account">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The biblical account about David comes from the
      <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Samuel" title="Books of Samuel">
       Books of Samuel
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Chronicles" title="Books of Chronicles">
       Books of Chronicles
      </a>
      . Chronicles merely retells Samuel from a different theological vantage point, and contains little (if any) information not available there, and the biblical evidence for David is therefore dependent almost exclusively on the material contained in the chapters from 1 Samuel 16 to 1 Kings 2.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David-icon.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="451" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/David-icon.jpg/170px-David-icon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/David-icon.jpg/255px-David-icon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/David-icon.jpg/340px-David-icon.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David-icon.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_icon" title="Russian icon">
         Russian icon
        </a>
        of St. David, the Prophet and King, 18th century (
        <a href="/wiki/Iconostasis" title="Iconostasis">
         iconostasis
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Kizhi" title="Kizhi">
         Kizhi
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">
         monastery
        </a>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Since
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Noth" title="Martin Noth">
       Martin Noth
      </a>
      put forward his analysis of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deuteronomistic_history" title="Deuteronomistic history">
       Deuteronomistic history
      </a>
      , biblical scholars have accepted that these two books form part of a continuous history of Israel, compiled no earlier than the late 7th century BCE, but incorporating earlier works and fragments. Samuel's account of David "seems to have undergone two separate acts of editorial slanting". The original writers show a strong bias against Saul, and in favour of David and Solomon. Many years later the Deuteronomists edited the material in a manner that conveyed their religious message, inserting reports and anecdotes that strengthened their monotheistic doctrine. Some of the materials in Samuel I and IInotably the boundary, allotment and administrative listsare believed to be very early, since they correspond closely to what we know of the territorial conditions of the late Davidic-early Solomonic period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Beyond this, the full range of possible interpretations is available. The late
      <a href="/wiki/John_Bright_(biblical_scholar)" title="John Bright (biblical scholar)">
       John Bright
      </a>
      , in his
      <i>
       History of Israel
      </i>
      takes Samuel at face value.
      <a href="/wiki/Donald_B._Redford" title="Donald B. Redford">
       Donald B. Redford
      </a>
      , however, thinks all reconstructions from biblical sources for the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Israel_(united_monarchy)" title="Kingdom of Israel (united monarchy)">
       United Monarchy
      </a>
      period are examples of "academic wishful thinking".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_L._Thompson" title="Thomas L. Thompson">
       Thomas L. Thompson
      </a>
      rejects the historicity of the biblical narrative, "The history of Palestine and of its peoples is very different from the Bible's narratives, whatever political claims to the contrary may be. An independent history of Judea during the Iron I and Iron II periods has little room for historicizing readings of the stories of I-II Samuel and I Kings."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Amihai_Mazar" title="Amihai Mazar">
       Amihai Mazar
      </a>
      however, concludes that based on recent archeological findings, like those in
      <a href="/wiki/City_of_David" title="City of David">
       City of David
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Khirbet_Qeiyafa" title="Khirbet Qeiyafa">
       Khirbet Qeiyafa
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tel_Dan" title="Tel Dan">
       Tel Dan
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tel_Rehov" title="Tel Rehov">
       Tel Rehov
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Khirbet_en-Nahas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Khirbet en-Nahas (page does not exist)">
       Khirbet en-Nahas
      </a>
      and others "the deconstruction of United Monarchy and the devaluation of Judah as a state in 9th century is unacceptable interpretation of available historic data". According to Mazar, based on archeological evidences,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Monarchy" title="United Monarchy">
       United Monarchy
      </a>
      can be described as a "state in development".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some studies of David have been written:
      <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Halpern" title="Baruch Halpern">
       Baruch Halpern
      </a>
      has pictured David as a lifelong vassal of
      <a href="/wiki/Achish" title="Achish">
       Achish
      </a>
      , the Philistine king of Gath;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Israel_Finkelstein" title="Israel Finkelstein">
       Israel Finkelstein
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Neil_Asher_Silberman" title="Neil Asher Silberman">
       Neil Asher Silberman
      </a>
      have identified as the oldest and most reliable section of Samuel those chapters which describe David as the charismatic leader of a band of outlaws who captures Jerusalem and makes it his capital.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Steven McKenzie, Associate Professor of the Hebrew Bible at
      <a href="/wiki/Rhodes_College" title="Rhodes College">
       Rhodes College
      </a>
      and author of
      <i>
       King David: A Biography
      </i>
      , states the belief that David actually came from a wealthy family, was "ambitious and ruthless" and a tyrant who murdered his opponents, including his own sons.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenzie_on_David_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_on_David-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Critical Bible scholarship holds that the biblical account of Davids rise to power is a political apologyan answer to contemporary charges against him, of his involvement in murders and regicide.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Jacob L. Wright, Associate Professor of Hebrew Bible at Emory University, has written that the most popular legends about David, including his killing of Goliath, his affair with Bathsheba, and his ruling of a United Kingdom of Israel rather than just Judah, are the creation of those who lived generations after him, in particular those living in the late Persian or Hellenistic period.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_descriptions">
       Physical descriptions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Physical descriptions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      1 Samuel 16:12 "And he sent and brought him in. Now he was ruddy and had beautiful eyes and was handsome. And the LORD said, "Arise, anoint him, for this is he." - Holy Bible; English Standard Version.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      1 Samuel 17:41-43 "And the Philistine moved forward and came near to David, with his shield-bearer in front of him. And when the Philistine looked and saw David, he disdained him, for he was but a youth, ruddy and handsome in appearance."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Hebrew word for 'ruddy' used in the above passages is
      <i>
       admoni
      </i>
      (), from the root ADM (, see also
      <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">
       Adam
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Edom" title="Edom">
       Edom
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "Admoni", reddish-brown, was the ideal colour for men, and indicates David's heroic nature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETsumura2007423_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETsumura2007423-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite the fact his hair is not mentioned in the passages, the description led to a later Sephardic and Ashkenazi tradition that David was a red-head.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Abrahamic_religious_traditions">
       Abrahamic religious traditions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Abrahamic religious traditions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em">
      <tr>
       <th class="fn" colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color:gold;">

       </th>
      </tr>
