     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="David_as_Psalmist">
       David as Psalmist
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: David as Psalmist">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      While almost half of the
      <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">
       Psalms
      </a>
      are headed "A Psalm of David" (though the phrase can also be translated as "to David" or "for David") and tradition identifies several with specific events in Davids life (e.g.,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_3" title="Psalm 3">
       Psalms 3
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_7" title="Psalm 7">
       7
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_18" title="Psalm 18">
       18
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_34" title="Psalm 34">
       34
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_51" title="Psalm 51">
       51
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_52" title="Psalm 52">
       52
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_54" title="Psalm 54">
       54
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_56" title="Psalm 56">
       56
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_57" title="Psalm 57">
       57
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_59" title="Psalm 59">
       59
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_60" title="Psalm 60">
       60
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_63" title="Psalm 63">
       63
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Psalm_142" title="Psalm 142">
       142
      </a>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the headings are late additions and no psalm can be attributed to David with certainty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKenzie_on_David_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_on_David-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Psalm 34 is attributed to David on the occasion of his escape from the Abimelech (king)
      <a href="/wiki/Achish" title="Achish">
       Achish
      </a>
      by pretending to be insane.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the narrative in 1 Samuel 21, instead of killing the man who had exacted so many casualties from him, Abimelech allows David to depart, exclaiming, "Am I so short of madmen that you have to bring this fellow here to carry on like this in front of me? Must this man come into my house?"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">
       Judaism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Judaism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      David is an important figure in Judaism. Historically, David's reign represented the formation of a coherent
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Monarchy" title="United Monarchy">
       Jewish kingdom
      </a>
      centered in
      <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">
       Jerusalem
      </a>
      . David is an important figure within the context of
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_messianism" title="Jewish messianism">
       Jewish messianism
      </a>
      . In the
      <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">
       Hebrew Bible
      </a>
      , it is written that a human descendant of David will occupy the throne of a restored kingdom and usher in a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messianic_age" title="Messianic age">
       messianic age
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      David is also viewed as a tragic figure; his acquisition of Bathsheba, and the loss of his son are viewed as his central tragedies.
     </p>
     <p>
      Many legends have grown around the figure of David. According to one Rabbinic tradition, David was raised as the son of his father Jesse and spent his early years herding his father's sheep in the wilderness while his brothers were in school. Only at his anointing by Samuelwhen the oil from Samuel's flask turned to diamonds and pearlswas his true identity as Jesse's son revealed.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      David's adultery with Bathsheba was only an opportunity to demonstrate the power of repentance, and the Talmud states that it was not adultery at all, quoting a Jewish practice of divorce on the eve of battle. Furthermore, according to Talmudic sources, the death of Uriah was not to be considered murder, on the basis that Uriah had committed a capital offence by refusing to obey a direct command from the King.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, in tractate Sanhedrin, David expressed remorse over his transgressions and sought forgiveness. God ultimately forgave David and Bathsheba but would not remove their sins from Scripture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midrashim" title="Midrashim">
       midrashim
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Adam" title="Adam">
       Adam
      </a>
      gave up 70 years of his life for the life of David.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Also, according to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talmud_Yerushalmi" title="Talmud Yerushalmi">
       Talmud Yerushalmi
      </a>
      , David was born and died on the Jewish holiday of
      <a href="/wiki/Shavuot" title="Shavuot">
       Shavuot
      </a>
      (Feast of Weeks). His piety was said to be so great that his prayers could bring down things from Heaven.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2397" data-file-width="3000" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg/260px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg/390px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg/520px-Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg 2x" width="260"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rembrandt_Harmensz._van_Rijn_030.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Saul and David
        </i>
        , by
        <a href="/wiki/Rembrandt" title="Rembrandt">
         Rembrandt
        </a>
        , c. 1650. David plays the lyre (depicted here as a
        <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">
         harp
        </a>
        ) to the king tormented by an evil spirit.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Christianity">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">
       Genealogy of Jesus
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The concept of the Messiah is important in Christianity. Originally an earthly king ruling by divine appointment ("the anointed one", as the title
      <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">
       Messiah
      </a>
      had it), the "son of David" became in the last two pre-Christian centuries the apocalyptic and heavenly one who would deliver Israel and usher in a new kingdom. This was the background to the concept of Messiahship in early Christianity, which interpreted the career of Jesus "by means of the titles and functions assigned to David in the mysticism of the Zion cult, in which he served as priest-king and in which he was the mediator between God and man".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The early Church believed that "the life of David [foreshadowed] the life of Christ;
      <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem" title="Bethlehem">
       Bethlehem
      </a>
      is the birthplace of both; the shepherd life of David points out Christ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Good_Shepherd_(Christianity)" title="The Good Shepherd (Christianity)">
       the Good Shepherd
      </a>
      ; the five stones chosen to slay Goliath are
      <a href="/wiki/Typology_(theology)" title="Typology (theology)">
       typical
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Wounds" title="Holy Wounds">
       five wounds
      </a>
      ; the betrayal by his trusted counsellor,
      <a href="/wiki/Ahitophel" title="Ahitophel">
       Achitophel
      </a>
      , and the passage over the
      <a href="/wiki/Kidron_Valley" title="Kidron Valley">
       Cedron
      </a>
      remind us of Christ's
      <a href="/wiki/Passion_(Christianity)" title="Passion (Christianity)">
       Sacred Passion
      </a>
      . Many of the Davidic Psalms, as we learn from the New Testament, are clearly
      <a href="/wiki/Typology_(theology)" title="Typology (theology)">
       typical
      </a>
      of the future
      <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">
       Messiah
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-corbett_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-corbett-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
       Middle Ages
      </a>
      , "
      <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
       Charlemagne
      </a>
      thought of himself, and was viewed by his court scholars, as a 'new David'. [This was] not in itself a new idea, but [one whose] content and significance were greatly enlarged by him".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The linking of David to earthly kingship was reflected in later Medieval cathedral windows all over Europe through the device of the
      <a href="/wiki/Tree_of_Jesse" title="Tree of Jesse">
       Tree of Jesse
      </a>
      , its branches demonstrating how divine kingship descended from Jesse, through his son David, to Jesus.
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Latin_liturgical_rites" title="Latin liturgical rites">
       Western Rite
      </a>
      churches (Lutheran, Roman Catholic) celebrate his feast day on 29 December, Eastern-rite on 19 December.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">
       Eastern Orthodox Church
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic_Churches" title="Eastern Catholic Churches">
       Eastern Catholic Churches
      </a>
      celebrate the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_day" title="Feast day">
       feast day
      </a>
      of the "Holy Righteous Prophet and King David" on the Sunday of the Holy Forefathers (two Sundays before the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Feast" title="Great Feast">
       Great Feast
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">
       Nativity of the Lord
      </a>
      ), when he is commemorated together with other
      <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_of_Jesus" title="Genealogy of Jesus">
       ancestors of Jesus
      </a>
      . He is also commemorated on the Sunday after the Nativity, together with
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Joseph" title="Saint Joseph">
       Joseph
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James,_the_Brother_of_the_Lord" title="James, the Brother of the Lord">
       James, the Brother of the Lord
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Latter_Day_Saints">
       Latter Day Saints
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Latter Day Saints">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Latter_Day_Saint_movement" title="Latter Day Saint movement">
       Latter Day Saint movement
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Mormon" title="Book of Mormon">
       Book of Mormon
      </a>
      offers a negative commentary on David's practice of
      <a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">
       polygamy
      </a>
      . In the
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jacob" title="Book of Jacob">
       Book of Jacob
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Nephite" title="Nephite">
       Nephite
      </a>
      nation begins to practice polygamy, justifying it by the example of David and Solomon. In response the prophet
      <a href="/wiki/Jacob_(Book_of_Mormon_prophet)" title="Jacob (Book of Mormon prophet)">
       Jacob
      </a>
      denounces both David's taking of "many wives"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the Nephites' taking of multiple wives,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though he stops short of denouncing polygamy altogether.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Editions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Doctrine_and_Covenants" title="Doctrine and Covenants">
       Doctrine and Covenants
      </a>
      utilized by
      <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">
       The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints
      </a>
      , the largest Latter Day Saint denomination, state that of David's sexual relationships, only his relationship with Bathsheba was a sin. However, in consequence of this sin and the further sin of killing Uriah, David had "fallen from
      <a href="/wiki/Exaltation_(Mormonism)" title="Exaltation (Mormonism)">
       exaltation
      </a>
      " and would not be married to any of his wives in the next life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foundation_for_Apologetic_Information_%26_Research" title="Foundation for Apologetic Information &amp; Research">
       Foundation for Apologetic Information &amp; Research
      </a>
      argues that there is no contradiction between the Book of Mormon and Doctrine and Covenants because the Lord authorized David to have some wives, but not "many" wives. They see a parallel between
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.lds.org/scriptures/bofm/jacob/2.24?lang=eng#23" rel="nofollow">
       Jacob 2:24
      </a>
      and
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Deuteronomy&amp;verse=17:17&amp;src=KJV">
       Deuteronomy 17:17
      </a>
      , which some rabbis interpreted as a limit of four wives per husband. When David took Bathsheba, he crossed the line into having "many" wives, which he was not authorized to do. Jacob's denunciation then becomes, not a complete denunciation of David's polygamy, but a denunciation of unauthorized indulgence in polygamy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Christ" title="Community of Christ">
       Community of Christ
      </a>
      , the second-largest Latter Day Saint faction, does not accept the validity of 132nd section of the LDS Doctrine and Covenants; nor does the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Christ_(Temple_Lot)" title="Church of Christ (Temple Lot)">
       Church of Christ (Temple Lot)
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Jesus_Christ_(Cutlerite)" title="Church of Jesus Christ (Cutlerite)">
       Church of Jesus Christ (Cutlerite)
      </a>
      , and many other smaller factions. Although the
      <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter_Day_Saints_(Strangite)" title="Church of Jesus Christ of Latter Day Saints (Strangite)">
       Church of Jesus Christ of Latter Day Saints (Strangite)
      </a>
      accepted the validity of polygamy as an institution, they do not accept Doctrine and Covenants 132, nor do they believe that Joseph Smith instituted or taught it (they believe that
      <a href="/wiki/James_Strang" title="James Strang">
       James Strang
      </a>
      was responsible for that, when he released his
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_the_Law_of_the_Lord" title="Book of the Law of the Lord">
       Book of the Law of the Lord
      </a>
      in 1850).
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">
       Islam
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Islam">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/David_in_Islam" title="David in Islam">
       David in Islam
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      David (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
       Arabic
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       Dwd
      </i>
      ) is a highly important figure in
      <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
       Islam
      </a>
      as one of the major
      <a href="/wiki/Prophet" title="Prophet">
       prophets
      </a>
      sent by
      <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
       God
      </a>
      to guide the
      <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">
       Israelites
      </a>
      . David is mentioned several times in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">
       Qur'an
      </a>
      , often with his son
      <a href="/wiki/Solomon" title="Solomon">
       Solomon
      </a>
      . The actual Arabic equivalent to the Hebrew Davd is Dawd. In the Qur'an: David killed Goliath (2:251), Goliath was a powerful king who used to invade random kingdoms and villages. Goliath was spreading evil and corruption. When David killed Goliath, God granted him kingship and wisdom and enforces it (38:20). David is made God's "
      <a href="/wiki/Vicegerent" title="Vicegerent">
       vicegerent
      </a>
      on earth" (38:26) and God further gives David sound judgment (21:78; 37:2124, 26) as well as the
      <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">
       Psalms
      </a>
      , which are regarded as books of divine wisdom (4:163; 17:55). The birds and mountains unite with David in uttering praise to God (21:79; 34:10; 38:18), while God made iron soft for David (
      <a class="external text" href="http://quran.com/34/10," rel="nofollow">
       34:10
      </a>
      ), God also instructed David in the art of fashioning chain-mail out of iron (
      <a class="external text" href="http://quran.com/21/80" rel="nofollow">
       21:80
      </a>
      ); an indication of the first use of
      <a href="/wiki/Wrought_iron" title="Wrought iron">
       Wrought iron
      </a>
      , this knowledge gave David a major advantage over his
      <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">
       bronze
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">
       cast iron
      </a>
      -armed opponents, not to mention the cultural and economic impact. Together with Solomon, David gives judgment in a case of damage to the fields (21:78) and David judges in the matter between two disputants in his prayer chamber (38:2123). Since there is no mention in the Qur'an of the wrong David did to
      <a href="/wiki/Uriah_the_Hittite" title="Uriah the Hittite">
       Uriah
      </a>
      nor is there any reference to
      <a href="/wiki/Bathsheba" title="Bathsheba">
       Bathsheba
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
       Muslims
      </a>
      reject this narrative.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Muslim
      <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">
       tradition
      </a>
      and the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">
        hadith
       </a>
      </i>
      stress David's zeal in daily prayer as well as in
      <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">
       fasting
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Qur'an commentators, historians and compilers of the numerous
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stories_of_the_Prophets" title="Stories of the Prophets">
        Stories of the Prophets
       </a>
      </i>
      elaborate upon David's concise Qur'anic narratives and specifically mention David's gift in singing his Psalms as well as his musical and vocal talents. His voice is described as having had a captivating power, weaving its influence not only over man but over all beasts and nature, who would unite with him to praise God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Baha.27i_Faith">
       Baha'i Faith
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Baha'i Faith">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baha%27i_Faith" title="Baha'i Faith">
       Baha'i Faith
      </a>
      , David is described as a reflection of God and one among a long line of prophets who came in the shadow of the
      <a href="/wiki/Dispensationalism" title="Dispensationalism">
       dispensation
      </a>
      of Moses to develop and consolidate the process he set in motion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifestation_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-manifestation-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Kit%C3%A1b-i-%C3%8Dq%C3%A1n" title="Kitb-i-qn">
       Kitb-i-qn
      </a>
      describes David as being "among the more exalted Manifestations who have appeared during the intervening period between the revelations of Moses and Muhammad, ever altered the law of the Qiblih".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legend_and_legacy">
       Legend and legacy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Legend and legacy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_Ireland_(Variant_1)_(Historical).svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="478" data-file-width="410" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Arms_of_Ireland_%28Variant_1%29_%28Historical%29.svg/150px-Arms_of_Ireland_%28Variant_1%29_%28Historical%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Arms_of_Ireland_%28Variant_1%29_%28Historical%29.svg/225px-Arms_of_Ireland_%28Variant_1%29_%28Historical%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Arms_of_Ireland_%28Variant_1%29_%28Historical%29.svg/300px-Arms_of_Ireland_%28Variant_1%29_%28Historical%29.svg.png 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arms_of_Ireland_(Variant_1)_(Historical).svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Coat of arms
        <a href="/wiki/Attributed_arms" title="Attributed arms">
         attributed
        </a>
        to King David by mediaeval heralds
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
         <a href="#cite_note-66">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          66
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        (coincidentally identical to the arms of
        <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">
         Ireland
        </a>
        )
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In European Christian culture of the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
       Middle Ages
      </a>
      , David was made a member of the
      <a href="/wiki/Nine_Worthies" title="Nine Worthies">
       Nine Worthies
      </a>
      , a group of heroes encapsulating all the ideal qualities of
      <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">
       chivalry
      </a>
      . His life was thus proposed as a valuable subject for study by those aspiring to chivalric status. This aspect of David in the Nine Worthies was popularised firstly through literature, and was thereafter adopted as a frequent subject for painters and sculptors.
     </p>
     <p>
      David was considered as a model ruler and a symbol of the God-ordained monarchy throughout medieval Western Europe and Eastern Christendom. David was perceived as the biblical predecessor to Christian Roman and Byzantine emperors and the name "New David" was used as an honorific reference to these rulers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garipzanov_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Garipzanov-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Georgia" title="Kingdom of Georgia">
       Georgian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bagrationi" title="Bagrationi">
       Bagratids
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Solomonic_dynasty" title="Solomonic dynasty">
       Solomonic dynasty
      </a>
      of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_of_Ethiopia" title="Empire of Ethiopia">
       Ethiopia
      </a>
      claimed a direct
      <a href="/wiki/Claim_of_the_biblical_descent_of_the_Bagrationi_dynasty" title="Claim of the biblical descent of the Bagrationi dynasty">
       biological descent
      </a>
      from him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Likewise, the
      <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">
       Frankish
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">
       Carolingian dynasty
      </a>
      frequently connected themselves to David;
      <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
       Charlemagne
      </a>
      himself occasionally used the name of David as his pseudonym.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garipzanov_67-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Garipzanov-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Representation_in_art_and_literature">
       Representation in art and literature
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Representation in art and literature">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Art">
       Art
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Art">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Early Byzantine Depictions of David include (in chronological order):
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/David_Plates" title="David Plates">
        David Plates
       </a>
       (c. 613  630)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Paris_Psalter" title="Paris Psalter">
        Paris Psalter
       </a>
       (c. 950)
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Chludov_Psalter" title="Chludov Psalter">
        Chludov Psalter
       </a>
       (c. 843)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Famous sculptures of David include (in chronological order) those by:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Donatello" title="Donatello">
        Donatello
       </a>
       (c. 1430  1440),
       <a href="/wiki/David_(Donatello)" title="David (Donatello)">
        <i>
         David
        </i>
        (Donatello)
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Andrea_del_Verrocchio" title="Andrea del Verrocchio">
        Andrea del Verrocchio
       </a>
       (1476),
       <a href="/wiki/David_(Verrocchio)" title="David (Verrocchio)">
        <i>
         David
        </i>
        (Verrocchio)
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">
        Michelangelo
       </a>
       (1504),
       <a href="/wiki/David_(Michelangelo)" title="David (Michelangelo)">
        <i>
         David
        </i>
        (Michelangelo)
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Gian_Lorenzo_Bernini" title="Gian Lorenzo Bernini">
        Gian Lorenzo Bernini
       </a>
       (1624),
       <a href="/wiki/David_(Bernini)" title="David (Bernini)">
        <i>
         David
        </i>
        (Bernini)
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Antonin_Merci%C3%A9" title="Antonin Merci">
        Antonin Merci
       </a>
       (1873)
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
       Literature
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Literature">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">
        Dryden
       </a>
       's long poem
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Absalom_and_Achitophel" title="Absalom and Achitophel">
         Absalom and Achitophel
        </a>
       </i>
       is an allegory that uses the story of the rebellion of
       <a href="/wiki/Absalom" title="Absalom">
        Absalom
       </a>
       against King David as the basis for his satire of the contemporary political situation, including events such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Monmouth_Rebellion" title="Monmouth Rebellion">
        Monmouth Rebellion
       </a>
       (1685), the
       <a href="/wiki/Popish_Plot" title="Popish Plot">
        Popish Plot
       </a>
       (1678) and the
       <a href="/wiki/Exclusion_Crisis" title="Exclusion Crisis">
        Exclusion Crisis
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Elmer_Davis" title="Elmer Davis">
        Elmer Davis
       </a>
       's 1928 novel
       <i>
        Giant Killer
       </i>
       retells and embellishes the Biblical story of David, casting David as primarily a poet who managed always to find others to do the "dirty work" of heroism and kingship. In the novel,
       <a href="/wiki/Elhanan_son_of_Jair" title="Elhanan son of Jair">
        Elhanan
       </a>
       in fact killed Goliath but David claimed the credit; and
       <a href="/wiki/Joab" title="Joab">
        Joab
       </a>
       , David's cousin and general, took it upon himself to make many of the difficult decisions of war and statecraft when David vacillated or wrote poetry instead
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Sir Arthur Conan Doyle">
        Sir Arthur Conan Doyle
       </a>
       used the story of David and Bathsheba as the main structure for the
       <a href="/wiki/Sherlock_Holmes" title="Sherlock Holmes">
        Sherlock Holmes
       </a>
       story
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Adventure_of_the_Crooked_Man" title="The Adventure of the Crooked Man">
         The Adventure of the Crooked Man
        </a>
       </i>
       . The betrayal of the Crooked Man is paralleled with David's betrayal of Uriah the Hittite, carried out in order to win Bathsheba
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">
        William Faulkner
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Absalom,_Absalom!" title="Absalom, Absalom!">
         Absalom, Absalom!
        </a>
       </i>
       (1936) refers to the story of Absalom, David's son; his rebellion against his father and his death at the hands of David's general, Joab. In addition it parallels Absalom's vengeance for the rape of his sister Tamar by his half-brother, Amnon.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Gladys_Schmitt" title="Gladys Schmitt">
        Gladys Schmitt
       </a>
       's 1946 novel
       <i>
        David the King
       </i>
       was a richly embellished biography of David's entire life. The book took a risk, especially for its time, in portraying David's relationship with Jonathan as overtly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homoerotic" title="Homoerotic">
        homoerotic
       </a>
       , but was ultimately panned by critics as a bland rendition of the title character
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Juan_Bosch" title="Juan Bosch">
        Juan Bosch
       </a>
       , a Dominican political leader and writer, wrote
       <i>
        David: Biography of a King
       </i>
       in 1966, as a realistic portrayal of David's life and political career
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Dan_Jacobson" title="Dan Jacobson">
        Dan Jacobson
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        The Rape of Tamar
       </i>
       (1970) is an imagined account, by one of David's courtiers Yonadab, of the rape of Tamar by Amnon
      </li>
      <li>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Burnett_Swann" title="Thomas Burnett Swann">
        Thomas Burnett Swann
       </a>
       's 1974 Biblical fantasy novel
       <i>
        How are the Mighty Fallen
       </i>
       , David and Jonathan are explicitly stated to be lovers. Moreover, Jonathan is a member of a winged semi-human race (possibly
       <a href="/wiki/Nephilim" title="Nephilim">
        nephilim
       </a>
       ), one of several such races coexisting with humanity but often persecuted by it
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Malachi_Martin" title="Malachi Martin">
        Malachi Martin
       </a>
       's 1980
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faction_(literature)" title="Faction (literature)">
        factional
       </a>
       novel
       <i>
        King of Kings: A Novel of the Life of David
       </i>
       relates the life of David, Adonai's champion in his battle with the Philistine deity Dagon
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Heller" title="Joseph Heller">
        Joseph Heller
       </a>
       wrote a 1984 novel based on David called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/God_Knows_(novel)" title="God Knows (novel)">
         God Knows
        </a>
       </i>
       , published by Simon &amp; Schuster. Told from the perspective of an aging David, the humanityrather than the heroismof various biblical characters is emphasized. The portrayal of David as a man of flaws such as greed, lust, selfishness, and his alienation from God, the falling apart of his family is a distinctly 20th-century interpretation of the events told in the Bible
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Madeleine_L%27Engle" title="Madeleine L'Engle">
        Madeleine L'Engle
       </a>
       's 1993 novel
       <i>
        Certain Women
       </i>
       explores family, the Christian faith, and the nature of God through the story of King David's family and an analogous modern family's saga
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Allan_Massie" title="Allan Massie">
        Allan Massie
       </a>
       wrote
       <i>
        King David
       </i>
       , a 1996 novel about David's career that portrays the king's relationship to Jonathan and others as openly
       <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">
        homosexual
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Heym" title="Stefan Heym">
        Stefan Heym
       </a>
       wrote
       <i>
        The King David Report
       </i>
       , a work of fiction published in 1998 by the Northwestern University Press depicting the writings of the Bible historian Ethan, upon King Solomon's orders, of a true and authoritative report on the life of David, Son of Jesse
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Film">

      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">

      <li>

       </a>
       portrayed King David in the 2013 miniseries
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Bible_(TV_series)" title="The Bible (TV series)">
         The Bible
        </a>
       </i>
       produced by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mark_Burnett_(executive_producer)" title="Mark Burnett (executive producer)">
        Mark Burnett
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Roma_Downey" title="Roma Downey">
        Roma Downey
       </a>
       .
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Music">
       Music
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Music">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Josquin_des_Prez" title="Josquin des Prez">
        Josquin des Prez
       </a>
       's Planxit autem David is a polyphonic setting of 2 Samuel, chapter one verses 1727, David's lamentation for the dead Saul and Jonathan. His Absalon fili mi is a polyphonic lamentation from David's perspective on the death of his son.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/George_Frideric_Handel" title="George Frideric Handel">
        George Frideric Handel
       </a>
       's oratorio
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Saul_(Handel)" title="Saul (Handel)">
         Saul
        </a>
       </i>
       features David as one of its main characters
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handel_Institute_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Handel_Institute-69">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         69
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Honegger" title="Arthur Honegger">
        Arthur Honegger
       </a>
       's oratorio
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le_roi_David_(Honegger)" title="Le roi David (Honegger)">
         Le Roi David
        </a>
       </i>
       ("King David"), with a libretto by Rene Morax, was composed in 1921 and instantly became a staple of the choral repertoire; it is still widely performed
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Cohen" title="Leonard Cohen">
        Leonard Cohen
       </a>
       's song "
       <a href="/wiki/Hallelujah_(Leonard_Cohen_song)" title="Hallelujah (Leonard Cohen song)">
        Hallelujah
       </a>
       " (1984) has references to David ("there was a secret chord that David played and it pleased the Lord", "The baffled king composing Hallelujah") and
       <a href="/wiki/Bathsheba" title="Bathsheba">
        Bathsheba
       </a>
       ("you saw her bathing on the roof") in its opening verses
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Pixies" title="The Pixies">
        The Pixies
       </a>
       ' song "
       <a href="/wiki/Doolittle_(album)" title="Doolittle (album)">
        Dead
       </a>
       " on
       <a href="/wiki/Doolittle_(album)" title="Doolittle (album)">
        <i>
         Doolittle
        </i>
       </a>
       (1989) is a retelling of David's adultery and repentance
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The song "One of the Broken" by
       <a href="/wiki/Paddy_McAloon" title="Paddy McAloon">
        Paddy McAloon
       </a>
       , performed by
       <a href="/wiki/Prefab_Sprout" title="Prefab Sprout">
        Prefab Sprout
       </a>
       on the 1990 album
       <i>
        Jordan: The Comeback
       </i>
       , has a reference to David ("I remember King David, with his harp and his beautiful, beautiful songs, I answered his prayers, and showed him a place where his music belongs").
      </li>
      <li>
       "Mad About You", a song on
       <a href="/wiki/Sting_(musician)" title="Sting (musician)">
        Sting's
       </a>
       1991 album
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/The_Soul_Cages" title="The Soul Cages">
         The Soul Cages
        </a>
       </i>
       , explores David's obsession with Bathsheba from David's perspective
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Eric_Whitacre" title="Eric Whitacre">
        Eric Whitacre
       </a>
       composed a choral piece, "When David Heard" (1999), chronicling the death of Absalom and David's grief over losing his son
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The Song "Gimme a Stone" appears on the
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Feat" title="Little Feat">
        Little Feat
       </a>
       2000 album
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Work_Songs" title="Chinese Work Songs">
         Chinese Work Songs
        </a>
       </i>
       chronicles the duel with Goliath and contains a lament to Absalom as a bridge.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       "The Angel of Death Came to David's Room" (2009) by
       <a href="/wiki/MewithoutYou" title="MewithoutYou">
        MewithoutYou
       </a>
       is in reference to King David
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       "Your Heart" by
       <a href="/wiki/Chris_Tomlin" title="Chris Tomlin">
        Chris Tomlin
       </a>
       on
       <i>
        Music inspired by The Story
       </i>
       (2011) is a prayer of David
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_theater">
       Musical theater
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Musical theater">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/King_David_(musical)" title="King David (musical)">
        King David
       </a>
       , a modern oratorio, with a book and lyrics by
       <a href="/wiki/Tim_Rice" title="Tim Rice">
        Tim Rice
       </a>
       and music by
       <a href="/wiki/Alan_Menken" title="Alan Menken">
        Alan Menken
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Television">
       Television
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Television">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <ul>
      <li>
       TV film
       <i>
        David
       </i>
       (1997), with
       <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Parker" title="Nathaniel Parker">
        Nathaniel Parker
       </a>
       portraying King David
      </li>
      <li>
       <a href="/wiki/Max_von_Sydow" title="Max von Sydow">
        Max von Sydow
       </a>
       portrayed an older King David in the TV film
       <i>
        Solomon
       </i>
       , a sequel to
       <i>
        David
       </i>
      </li>
      <li>
       The NBC series
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kings_(U.S._TV_series)" title="Kings (U.S. TV series)">
         Kings
        </a>
       </i>
       (2009), explicitly designed as a modern retelling of the David story
      </li>
      <li>
       The episode "
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Wishbone_episodes" title="List of Wishbone episodes">
        Little Big Dog
       </a>
       " of the PBS series
       <a href="/wiki/Wishbone_(TV_series)" title="Wishbone (TV series)">
        <i>
         Wishbone
        </i>
       </a>
       recounts the story of David, his favor with Saul, and his triumphant battle over Goliath
      </li>
      <li>
       The season two episode of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Xena:_Warrior_Princess" title="Xena: Warrior Princess">
         Xena: Warrior Princess
        </a>
       </i>
       "
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Xena:_Warrior_Princess_episodes" title="List of Xena: Warrior Princess episodes">
        Giant Killer
       </a>
       " features David and his killing of Goliath
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Playing_cards">
       Playing cards
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=David&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Playing cards">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      For a considerable period, starting in the 15th century and continuing until the 19th, French
      <a href="/wiki/Playing_card" title="Playing card">
       playing card
      </a>
      manufacturers assigned to each of the court cards names taken from history or mythology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In this context, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Spades" title="King of Spades">
       King of Spades
      </a>
      was often known as "David".
     </p>

         <tr>
          <td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2">
           <div>
            Note:
            <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
             Muslims
            </a>
            believe that there were many prophets sent by
            <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">
             God
            </a>
            to mankind. The
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prophets_in_Islam" title="Prophets in Islam">
             Islamic prophets
            </a>
            above are only the ones mentioned by name in the
            <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">
             Quran
            </a>
            .
           </div>
          </td>
         </tr>
